<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Liquefied natural gas</h1> <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div> <div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="jump-to-nav"></div> <a class="mw-jump-link" href="#mw-head">Jump to navigation</a>
<a class="mw-jump-link" href="#p-search">Jump to search</a>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Natural-gas_processing" title="Natural-gas processing">Natural-gas processing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Liquefied_petroleum_gas" title="Liquefied petroleum gas">Liquefied petroleum gas</a>.</div>
<p><b>Liquefied natural gas</b> (<b>LNG</b>) is <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas" title="Natural gas">natural gas</a> (predominantly <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, CH<sub>4</sub>, with some mixture of <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a> C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>6</sub>) that has been cooled down to liquid form for ease and safety of non-pressurized storage or transport. It takes up about 1/600th the volume of natural gas in the gaseous state (at <a href="/wiki/Standard_conditions_for_temperature_and_pressure" title="Standard conditions for temperature and pressure">standard conditions for temperature and pressure</a>).  It is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Odorless" title="Odorless">odorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Transparency_and_translucency" title="Transparency and translucency">colorless</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">non-toxic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Corrosive_substance" title="Corrosive substance">non-corrosive</a>. Hazards include flammability after vaporization into a gaseous state, freezing and <a href="/wiki/Asphyxia" title="Asphyxia">asphyxia</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Liquefaction_of_gases" title="Liquefaction of gases">liquefaction</a> process involves removal of certain components, such as dust, <a href="/wiki/Acid_gas" title="Acid gas">acid gases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helium" title="Helium">helium</a>, water, and heavy <a href="/wiki/Hydrocarbon" title="Hydrocarbon">hydrocarbons</a>, which could cause difficulty downstream. The natural gas is then <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensed</a> into a liquid at close to atmospheric pressure by cooling it to approximately −162 °C (−260 °F); maximum transport pressure is set at around 25 kPa (4 psi).
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:LNG_Process_En.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1037" data-file-width="663" height="469" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/LNG_Process_En.png/300px-LNG_Process_En.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/LNG_Process_En.png/450px-LNG_Process_En.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/LNG_Process_En.png/600px-LNG_Process_En.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:LNG_Process_En.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical LNG process. The gas is first extracted and transported to a processing plant where it is purified by removing any condensates such as water, oil, mud, as well as other gases such as CO<sub>2</sub> and H<sub>2</sub>S. An LNG process train will also typically be designed to remove trace amounts of mercury from the gas stream to prevent mercury amalgamizing with aluminium in the cryogenic heat exchangers. The gas is then cooled down in stages until it is liquefied. LNG is finally stored in storage tanks and can be loaded and shipped.</div></div></div>
<p>Natural gas is mainly converted to LNG for transport over the seas where laying pipelines is not feasible technically and economically.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> LNG achieves a higher reduction in volume than <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> (CNG) so that the (volumetric) <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> of LNG is 2.4 times greater than that of CNG (at 250 bar) or 60 percent that of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel fuel</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> This makes LNG cost efficient in marine transport over long distances. However, <a href="/wiki/CNG_carrier" title="CNG carrier">CNG carrier ships</a> can be used economically up to medium distances in marine transport.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> Specially designed <a href="/wiki/Cryogenics" title="Cryogenics">cryogenic</a> sea vessels (<a href="/wiki/LNG_carrier" title="LNG carrier">LNG carriers</a>) or cryogenic road tankers are used for LNG transport. LNG is principally used for transporting natural gas to markets, where it is <a href="/wiki/Regasification" title="Regasification">regasified</a> and distributed as pipeline natural gas. It can be used in <a href="/wiki/Natural_gas_vehicle" title="Natural gas vehicle">natural gas vehicles</a>, although it is more common to design vehicles to use CNG. LNG's relatively high cost of production and the need to store it in expensive cryogenic tanks have hindered widespread commercial use.  Despite these drawbacks, on energy basis LNG production is expected to hit 10% of the global crude production by 2020 (see <a href="#Trade">LNG Trade</a>).
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_energy_content_and_energy_density">Specific energy content and energy density</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liquefied_natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Specific energy content and energy density">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The heating value depends on the source of gas that is used and the process that is used to liquefy the gas. The range of heating value can span +/- 10 to 15 percent. A typical value of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higher_heating_value" title="Higher heating value">higher heating value</a> of LNG is approximately 50 MJ/kg or 21,500 BTU/lb.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> A typical value of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lower_heating_value" title="Lower heating value">lower heating value</a> of LNG is 45 MJ/kg or 19,350 BTU/lb.
</p><p>For the purpose of comparison of different fuels the heating value may be expressed in terms of energy per volume which is known as the <a href="/wiki/Energy_density" title="Energy density">energy density</a> expressed in MJ/litre. The density of LNG is roughly 0.41 kg/litre to 0.5 kg/litre, depending on temperature, pressure, and composition,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> compared to water at 1.0 kg/litre. Using the median value of 0.45 kg/litre, the typical energy density values are 22.5 MJ/litre (based on higher heating value) or 20.3 MJ/litre (based on lower heating value).
</p><p>The (volume-based) energy density of LNG is approximately 2.4 times greater than that of CNG which makes it economical to transport natural gas by ship in the form of LNG. The energy density of LNG is comparable to <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ethanol" title="Ethanol">ethanol</a> but is only 60 percent that of <a href="/wiki/Diesel_fuel" title="Diesel fuel">diesel</a> and 70 percent that of <a href="/wiki/Gasoline" title="Gasoline">gasoline</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liquefied_natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Experiments on the properties of gases started early in the seventeenth century. By the middle of the seventeenth century <a href="/wiki/Robert_Boyle" title="Robert Boyle">Robert Boyle</a> had derived the inverse relationship between the pressure and the volume of gases. About the same time, <a href="/wiki/Guillaume_Amontons" title="Guillaume Amontons">Guillaume Amontons</a> started looking into temperature effects on gas. Various gas experiments continued for the next 200 years. During that time there were efforts to liquefy gases. Many new facts on the nature of gases had been discovered. For example, early in the nineteenth century <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cagniard_de_la_Tour" title="Cagniard de la Tour">Cagniard de la Tour</a> had shown there was a temperature above which a gas could not be liquefied. There was a major push in the mid to late nineteenth century to liquefy all gases. A number of scientists including <a href="/wiki/Michael_Faraday" title="Michael Faraday">Michael Faraday</a>, <a href="/wiki/James_Prescott_Joule" title="James Prescott Joule">James Joule</a>, and <a href="/wiki/William_Thomson,_1st_Baron_Kelvin" title="William Thomson, 1st Baron Kelvin">William Thomson</a> (Lord Kelvin), did experiments in this area. In 1886 <a href="/wiki/Karol_Olszewski" title="Karol Olszewski">Karol Olszewski</a> liquefied methane, the primary constituent of natural gas. By 1900 all gases had been liquefied except <a href="/wiki/Liquid_helium" title="Liquid helium">helium</a> which was liquefied in 1908.
</p><p>The first large scale liquefaction of natural gas in the U.S. was in 1918 when the U.S. government liquefied natural gas as a way to extract helium, which is a small component of some natural gas. This helium was intended for use in British dirigibles for World War I. The liquid natural gas (LNG) was not stored, but regasified and immediately put into the gas mains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p><p>The key patents having to do with natural gas liquefaction were in 1915 and the mid-1930s. In 1915 <a href="/wiki/Godfrey_Lowell_Cabot" title="Godfrey Lowell Cabot">Godfrey Cabot</a> patented a method for storing liquid gases at very low temperatures. It consisted of a Thermos bottle type design which included a cold inner tank within an outer tank; the tanks being separated by insulation. In 1937 Lee Twomey received patents for a process for large scale liquefaction of natural gas. The intention was to store natural gas as a liquid so it could be used for shaving peak energy loads during cold snaps. Because of large volumes it is not practical to store natural gas, as a gas, near atmospheric pressure. However, if it can be liquefied it can be stored in a volume 600 times smaller. This is a practical way to store it but the gas must be stored at −260 °F (−162 °C).
</p><p>There are two processes for liquefying natural gas in large quantities. The first is the cascade process, in which the natural gas is cooled by another gas which in turn has been cooled by still another gas, hence named the "cascade" process. There are usually two cascade cycles prior to the liquid natural gas cycle. The other method is the <a href="/wiki/Hampson%E2%80%93Linde_cycle" title="Hampson–Linde cycle">Linde process</a>, with a variation of the Linde process, called the Claude process, being sometimes used. In this process, the gas is cooled regeneratively by continually passing it through an orifice until it is cooled to temperatures at which it liquefies. The cooling of gas by expanding it through an orifice was developed by James Joule and William Thomson and is known as the <a href="/wiki/Joule%E2%80%93Thomson_effect" title="Joule–Thomson effect">Joule–Thomson effect</a>. Lee Twomey used the cascade process for his patents.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Commercial_operations_in_the_United_States">Commercial operations in the United States</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liquefied_natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Commercial operations in the United States">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The East Ohio Gas Company built a full-scale commercial liquid natural gas (LNG) plant in Cleveland, Ohio, in 1940 just after a successful pilot plant built by its sister company, Hope Natural Gas Company of West Virginia. This was the first such plant in the world. Originally it had three spheres, approximately 63 feet in diameter containing LNG at −260 °F. Each sphere held the equivalent of about 50 million cubic feet of natural gas. A fourth tank, a cylinder, was added in 1942. It had an equivalent capacity of 100 million cubic feet of gas. The plant operated successfully for three years. The stored gas was regasified and put into the mains when cold snaps hit and extra capacity was needed. This precluded the denial of gas to some customers during a cold snap.
</p><p>The Cleveland plant failed on October 20, 1944 when the cylindrical tank ruptured spilling thousands of gallons of LNG over the plant and nearby neighborhood. The gas evaporated and caught fire, which caused 130 fatalities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The fire delayed further implementation of LNG facilities for several years. However, over the next 15 years new research on low-temperature alloys, and better insulation materials, set the stage for a revival of the industry. It restarted in 1959 when a U.S. World War II Liberty ship, the <i>Methane Pioneer</i>, converted to carry LNG, made a delivery of LNG from the U.S. Gulf coast to energy starved Great Britain. In June 1964, the world's first purpose-built LNG carrier, the "Methane Princess" entered service.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Soon after that a large natural gas field was discovered in Algeria. International trade in LNG quickly followed as LNG was shipped to France and Great Britain from the Algerian fields. One more important attribute of LNG had now been exploited. Once natural gas was liquefied it could not only be stored more easily, but it could be transported. Thus energy could now be shipped over the oceans via LNG the same way it was shipped by oil.
</p><p>The US LNG industry restarted in 1965 when a series of new plants were built in the U.S. The building continued through the 1970s. These plants were not only used for peak-shaving, as in Cleveland, but also for base-load supplies for places that never had natural gas prior to this. A number of import facilities were built on the East Coast in anticipation of the need to import energy via LNG. However, a recent boom in U.S. natural gas production (2010–2014), enabled by <a href="/wiki/Hydraulic_fracturing" title="Hydraulic fracturing">hydraulic fracturing</a> (“fracking”), has many of these import facilities being considered as export facilities. The first U.S. LNG export was completed in early 2016.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-bizjournals.com_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-bizjournals.com-10">[10]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liquefied_natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The natural gas fed into the LNG plant will be treated to remove water, <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a> and other components that will <a href="/wiki/Freezing" title="Freezing">freeze</a> (e.g., <a href="/wiki/Benzene" title="Benzene">benzene</a>) under the low temperatures needed for storage or be destructive to the liquefaction facility. LNG typically contains more than 90 percent <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. It also contains small amounts of <a href="/wiki/Ethane" title="Ethane">ethane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Propane" title="Propane">propane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Butane" title="Butane">butane</a>, some heavier <a href="/wiki/Alkane" title="Alkane">alkanes</a>, and nitrogen. The purification process can be designed to give almost 100 percent <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. One of the risks of LNG is <a href="/wiki/Rapid_phase_transition" title="Rapid phase transition">a rapid phase transition explosion</a> (RPT), which occurs when cold LNG comes into contact with <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The most important infrastructure needed for LNG production and transportation is an LNG plant consisting of one or more <a href="/wiki/LNG_train" title="LNG train">LNG trains</a>, each of which is an independent unit for gas liquefaction. The largest LNG train in operation is in Qatar, with a total production capacity of 7.8 million tonnes per annum (MTPA). These facilities recently reached a safety milestone, completing 12 years of operations on its offshore facilities without a Lost Time Incident.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The Qatar operation overtook the Train 4 of <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_LNG" title="Atlantic LNG">Atlantic LNG</a> in <a href="/wiki/Trinidad_and_Tobago" title="Trinidad and Tobago">Trinidad and Tobago</a> with a production capacity of 5.2 MTPA,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-upstream2_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-upstream2-13">[13]</a></sup> followed by the <a href="/wiki/SEGAS_LNG" title="SEGAS LNG">SEGAS LNG</a> plant in Egypt with a capacity of 5 MTPA. In July 2014, Atlantic LNG celebrated its 3000th cargo of LNG at the company’s liquefaction facility in Trinidad.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lngindustry.com_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-lngindustry.com-14">[14]</a></sup> The Qatargas II plant has a production capacity of 7.8 MTPA for each of its two trains. LNG sourced from Qatargas II will be supplied to Kuwait, following the signing of an agreement in May 2014 between Qatar <a class="external text" href="https://www.brothersgas.com/ig-overview/" rel="nofollow">Liquefied Gas Company</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kuwait_Petroleum_Corporation" title="Kuwait Petroleum Corporation">Kuwait Petroleum Corp</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lngindustry.com_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-lngindustry.com-14">[14]</a></sup> LNG is loaded onto ships and delivered to a regasification terminal, where the LNG is allowed to expand and reconvert into gas. Regasification terminals are usually connected to a storage and pipeline distribution network to distribute natural gas to local distribution companies (LDCs) or independent power plants (IPPs).
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="World_total_production">World total production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Liquefied_natural_gas&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: World total production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_LNG_Volumes.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1450" data-file-width="2100" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/World_LNG_Volumes.png/220px-World_LNG_Volumes.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/World_LNG_Volumes.png/330px-World_LNG_Volumes.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/World_LNG_Volumes.png/440px-World_LNG_Volumes.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_LNG_Volumes.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Global LNG import trends, by volume (in red), and as a percentage of global natural gas imports (in black) (US EIA data)</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1450" data-file-width="2100" height="152" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png/220px-Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png/330px-Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png/440px-Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Top_5_Importers_of_LNG.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Trends in the top five LNG-importing nations as of 2009 (US EIA data)</div></div></div>
